    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1293559805">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1293559805" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1143969-julie" class="left imgcol" title="Julie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409947508p2/1143969.jpg" alt="Julie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1293559805?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1143969-julie" class="user" title="Julie">Julie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/8117883-dune-messiah" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=futuristic" class="actionLinkLite">futuristic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1143969-julie?shelf=non-western-setting" class="actionLinkLite">non-western-setting</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1293559805">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7558264487422926524">Oh, Dune Messiah. We could've had it all. <em>We could've had it all.</em><br /><br />2.5-3 stars. Reviewing this book is hard, because it has such an interesting foundation, and in theory, I feel like I should have <em>loved</em> it -- in practice, however, the execution falls short. It takes place 12 years after the events of the first book, with Paul Atreides cemented as emperor and god-like figure, standing at the forefront of a jihad swarming across the galaxy and slaughtering billions in his name. Paul is repulsed by h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7558264487422926524">Oh, Dune Messiah. We could've had it all. <em>We could've had it all.</em><br /><br />2.5-3 stars. Reviewing this book is hard, because it has such an interesting foundation, and in theory, I feel like I should have <em>loved</em> it -- in practice, however, the execution falls short. It takes place 12 years after the events of the first book, with Paul Atreides cemented as emperor and god-like figure, standing at the forefront of a jihad swarming across the galaxy and slaughtering billions in his name. Paul is repulsed by his own holy war, backed into a corner, struggling to figure out how to disengage and find the path of least bloodshed, wishing for the good old days of simplicity, seeking a way out that won't just leave the perpetual motion machine churning on and grinding people up without him. He's older, wiser, and far more melancholic. He's more distant from people than ever, even including his concubine/love of his life Chani, his sister Alia, and his closest advisor Stilgar. Dune Messiah is about the prophet-emperor's downfall, the dangers of a god complex, the unstoppable influence of religion, and the impossibility of extracting yourself from power without sacrifice.<br /><br />All of which sounds awesome, right? But the book is a trudge: where Dune was war, vengeance, bildungsroman, and Shakespearean tragedy, Dune Messiah is plodding and meandering and philosophical. Not a lot actually happens, there's very little action, and I missed characters like Jessica, Liet-Kynes, Gurney, Leto, and the Emperor or Harkonnen villains. Paul is more inaccessible to the reader than ever, thanks to his entrenched near-godhood (most of his scenes seemed to consist of him staring ponderously off in a faraway psychic trance while someone tried and failed to get his attention and engage him in conversation tbh). Almost all of the conversations between people are so mysterious/allusive/indirect/philosophical that I never really felt like I knew what the hell they were talking about, their dialogue instead existing on another plane of symbolism and opaque reference. There's an interesting setup of attempted assassinations, various schemes set in motion against the Emperor, but the lack of action really bogs the plot down; the book is much shorter than Dune, but yet I was bored stiff throughout parts of it.<br /><br />To be entirely honest, I just sort of wish this book had been more like A Song of Ice and Fire, and featured scheming for the throne, assassination attempts, deals, betrayal, dark magic, and political machinations, but in a thrilling fashion. The first book was more like that.<br /><br />The things I did like: I was very interested in Hayt as a plotline, and Alia as a hormonal, struggling teenager appealed to me much more than her wunderkind toddlerdom in the previous book. Their romance wasn't written all that well though. Irulan is also an interesting character to me, a woman placed in a very tough spot, and I actually felt for her situation quite a bit. Also, the ending is great.<br /><br />Lots of favourite quotes below, because the philosophical meandering <em>is</em> very good for producing great lines, and Herbert's actual descriptive writing style is really nice sometimes -- it's just the plot itself that dragged.<br /><blockquote>
  <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[“How did you overcome your kwisatz haderach?” Irulan asked.<br />“A creature who has spent his life creating one particular representation of his selfdom will die rather than become the antithesis of that representation,” Scytale said.<br />“I do not understand,” Edric ventured.<br />“He killed himself,” the Reverend Mother growled.<br /><br />***<br /><br />“You think to steal the secret of it,” Mohiam wheezed. “And him with a planet guarded by his mad Fremen!”<br />“The Fremen are civil, educated and ignorant,” Scytale said. “They’re not mad. They’re trained to believe, not to know. Belief can be manipulated. Only knowledge is dangerous.”<br /><br />***<br /><br />But, putting on a stillsuit, he put on the desert. The suit with all its apparatus for reclaiming his body’s moisture guided his thoughts in subtle ways, fixed his movements in a desert pattern. He became wild Fremen. More than a disguise, the suit made of him a stranger to his city self. In the stillsuit, he abandoned security and put on the old skills of violence.<br /><br />***<br /><br /><br />Paul’s mouth went dry. For a moment, his nostrils tasted the smoke of a devastated future and the voice of another kind of vision commanding him to disengage . . . disengage . . . disengage. His prophetic visions had been eavesdropping on eternity for such a long while, catching snatches of foreign tongues, listening to stones and to flesh not his own. Since the day of his first encounter with terrible purpose, he had peered at the future, hoping to find peace.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Empires do not suffer emptiness of purpose at the time of their creation. It is when they have become established that aims are lost and replaced by vague ritual.<br /><br />***<br /><br />“Do you know what’s being said about your brother?” he demanded.<br />“I know what’s being said about your Qizarate,” Alia countered. “You’re not divines, you’re god’s spies.”<br />Korba glanced at Paul for support, said: “We are sent by the writ of Muad’dib, that He shall know the truth of His people and they shall know the truth of Him.”<br />“Spies,” Alia said.<br />Korba pursed his lips in injured silence.<br /><br />***<br /><br />“He didn’t kill them himself, Stil. He killed the way I kill, by sending out his legions. There’s another emperor I want you to note in passing—a Hitler. He killed more than six million. Pretty good for those days.”<br />“Killed . . . by his legions?” Stilgar asked.<br />“Yes.”<br />“Not very impressive statistics, m’Lord.”<br />“Very good, Stil.” Paul glanced at the reels in Korba’s hands. Korba stood with them as though he wished he could drop them and flee. “Statistics: at a conservative estimate, I’ve killed sixty-one billion, sterilized ninety planets, completely demoralized five hundred others. I’ve wiped out the followers of forty religions which had existed since—”<br />“Unbelievers!” Korba protested. “Unbelievers all!”<br />“No,” Paul said. “Believers.”<br />“My Liege makes a joke,” Korba said, voice trembling. “The Jihad has brought ten thousand worlds into the shining light of—”<br />“Into the darkness,” Paul said. “We’ll be a hundred generations recovering from Muad’dib’s Jihad. I find it hard to imagine that anyone will ever surpass this.” A barking laugh erupted from his throat.<br />“What amuses Muad’dib?” Stilgar asked.<br />“I am not amused. I merely had a sudden vision of the Emperor Hitler saying something similar. No doubt he did.”<br /><br />***<br /><br />Here lies a toppled god—<br />His fall was not a small one.<br />We did but build his pedestal,<br />A narrow and a tall one. <br />—TLEILAXU EPIGRAM<br /><br />***<br /><br />“And this is all you were thinking?”<br />“You know better than that, Alia.”<br />How dare he use my given name? She felt anger rise and go down beneath the memory of the way he’d spoken: softly throbbing undertones, casual male confidence. A muscle twitched along her jaw. She clenched her teeth.<br /><br />***<br /><br />“I told him that to endure oneself may be the hardest task in the universe.”<br />She shook her head. “That’s . . . that’s . . .”<br />“A bitter pill,” he said, watching the guards run toward them across the roof, taking up their escort positions.<br />“Bitter nonsense!”<br />“The greatest palatinate earl and the lowliest stipendiary serf share the same problem. You cannot hire a mentat or any other intellect to solve it for you. There’s no writ of inquest or calling of witnesses to provide answers. No servant—or disciple—can dress the wound. You dress it yourself or continue bleeding for all to see.”<br /><br />***<br /><br />Paul passed a hand across his forehead and eyes. The symbol-metropolis oppressed him. He despised his own thoughts. Such vacillation in another would have aroused his anger. He loathed his city! Rage rooted in boredom flickered and simmered deep within him, nurtured by decisions that couldn’t be avoided. He knew which path his feet must follow. He’d seen it enough times, hadn’t he? Seen it! Once . . . long ago, he’d thought of himself as an inventor of government. But the invention had fallen into old patterns. It was like some hideous contrivance with plastic memory. Shape it any way you wanted, but relax for a moment, and it snapped into the ancient forms. Forces at work beyond his reach in human breasts eluded and defied him.<br /><br />***<br /><br />She broke her gaze from Alia’s, feeling her own ambivalence and inadequacies. The pitfall of Bene Gesserit training, she reminded herself, lay in the powers granted: such powers predisposed one to vanity and pride. But power deluded those who used it. One tended to believe power could overcome any barrier . . . including one’s own ignorance.<br /><br />***<br /><br />“He didn’t use the Jihad,” Scytale said. “The Jihad used him. I think he would’ve stopped it if he could.”<br />“If he could? All he had to do was—”<br />“Oh, be still!” Scytale barked. “You can’t stop a mental epidemic. It leaps from person to person across parsecs. It’s overwhelmingly contagious. It strikes at the unprotected side, in the place where we lodge the fragments of other such plagues. Who can stop such a thing? Muad’dib hasn’t the antidote. The thing has roots in chaos. Can orders reach there?”<br />“Have you been infected, then?” Edric asked.<br /><br />***<br /><br />“Tell me what you’ve seen,” Chani said.<br />“I can’t.”<br />“Why mustn’t I kill her?”<br />“Because I ask it.” He watched her accept this. She did it the way sand accepted water: absorbing and concealing. Was there obedience beneath that hot, angry surface? he wondered. And he realized then that life in the royal Keep had left Chani unchanged. She’d merely stopped here for a time, inhabited a way station on a journey with her man. Nothing of the desert had been taken from her.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Paul found his attention caught by the signs above doorways, as though he were seeing them for the first time: Speed Merchants. Wind Stills and Retorts. Prophetic Prospects. Tests of Faith. Religious Supply. Weaponry . . . Propagation of the Faith . . . A more honest label would’ve been Propagation of the Bureaucracy, he thought. A type of religious civil servant had sprung up all through his universe. This new man of the Qizarate was more often a convert. He seldom displaced a Fremen in the key posts, but he was filling all the interstices. He used melange as much to show he could afford it as for the geriatric benefits. He stood apart from his rulers—Emperor, Guild, Bene Gesserit, Landsraad, Family or Qizarate. His gods were Routine and Records. He was served by mentats and prodigious filing systems. Expediency was the first word in his catechism, although he gave proper lip-service to the precepts of the Butlerians. Machines could not be fashioned in the image of a man’s mind, he said, but he betrayed by every action that he preferred machines to men, statistics to individuals, the faraway general view to the intimate personal touch requiring imagination and initiative.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Paul felt sickened. What are we doing? he asked himself. Alia was a child witch, but she was growing older. And he thought: Growing older is to grow more wicked.<br /><br />***<br /><br />She wasn’t really very old, Paul saw, but a look of lost hopes ringed her mouth, bitterness lay in her eyes.<br /><br />***<br /><br /><br />“He always speaks thus,” Otheym apologized.<br />“I don’t speak,” Bijaz said. “I operate a machine called language. It creaks and groans, but is mine own.”<br /><br />***<br /><br /><br />The convoluted wording of legalisms grew up around the necessity to hide from ourselves the violence we intend toward each other. Between depriving a man of one hour from his life and depriving him of his life there exists only a difference of degree.You have done violence to him, consumed his energy. Elaborate euphemisms may conceal your intent to kill, but behind any use of power over another the ultimate assumption remains: “I feed on your energy.”<br /><br />***<br /><br />“If I could only burn this thing out of me!” she cried. “I didn’t want to be different.”<br />“Please, Alia,” he murmured. “Let yourself sleep.”<br />“I wanted to be able to laugh,” she whispered. Tears slid down her cheeks. “But I’m sister to an Emperor who’s worshipped as a god. People fear me. I never wanted to be feared.”<br /><br />***<br /><br />She felt that the desert had followed her wherever she had gone. Coming back to the desert was not so much a homecoming as a turning around to see what had always been there.<br /><br />***<br /><br />“She is gone,” Paul said.<br />The ghola heard the words out of a blazing corona. They burned his chest, his backbone, the sockets of his metal eyes. He felt his right hand move toward the knife at his belt. His own thinking became strange, disjointed. He was a puppet held fast by strings reaching down from that awful corona. He moved to another’s commands, to another’s desires. The strings jerked his arms, his legs, his jaw. Sounds came squeezing out of his mouth, a terrifying repetitive noise— “Hraak! Hraak! Hraak!”<br />The knife came up to strike. In that instant, he grabbed his own voice, shaped rasping words: “Run! Young master, run!”<br />“We will not run,” Paul said. “We’ll move with dignity. We’ll do what must be done.”<br /><br />***<br /><br />This was more than a deathwatch, less than a wake. Paul felt his soul begging for respite, but still the vision moved him. Just a little farther now, he told himself. Black, visionless dark awaited him just ahead. There lay the place ripped out of the vision by grief and guilt, the place where the moon fell. He stumbled into it, would’ve fallen had Idaho not taken his arm in a fierce grip, a solid presence knowing how to share his grief in silence.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Paul felt himself accepting now the fact that Chani was dead. He had taken his place in a universe he did not want, wearing flesh that did not fit. Every breath he drew bruised his emotions. Two children! He wondered if he had committed himself to a passage where his vision would never return. It seemed unimportant.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Mentat, solve thyself, he thought.<br /><br />***<br /><br />The Bene Tleilax and the Guild had overplayed their hands and had lost, were discredited. The Qizarate was shaken by the treason of Korba and others high within it. And Paul’s final voluntary act, his ultimate acceptance of their customs, had ensured the loyalty of the Fremen to him and to his house. He was one of them forever now. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</blockquote><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]- <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_133744368">

  <a href="/user/show/3400470-wendy-lady-evelyn-quince" class="left imgcol" title="Wendy,  Lady Evelyn Quince"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1431958688p2/3400470.jpg" alt="Wendy,  Lady Evelyn Quince" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/133744368?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3400470-wendy-lady-evelyn-quince" class="user" title="Wendy,  Lady Evelyn Quince">Wendy,  Lady Evelyn Quince</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer133744368'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer133744368">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2599440494058985023">Because I am now reading "Children of Dune" and loving it (and crossing my fingers that it stays that way) I'm just putting up my old reading notes on "Dune Messiah" as a mish-mash that is not so much a review, just me trying to understand what went on.<br /><br />I tried several times to read this book and it was difficult from the outset. It's SO different from the philosophical yet action-packed "Dune," that it was jolting. It seemed less a sequel then an addendum. <br /><br />At first it reads too much like Herbert</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2599440494058985023">Because I am now reading "Children of Dune" and loving it (and crossing my fingers that it stays that way) I'm just putting up my old reading notes on "Dune Messiah" as a mish-mash that is not so much a review, just me trying to understand what went on.<br /><br />I tried several times to read this book and it was difficult from the outset. It's SO different from the philosophical yet action-packed "Dune," that it was jolting. It seemed less a sequel then an addendum. <br /><br />At first it reads too much like Herbert just scribbled down his thoughts while spaced out on acid. These moments of contemplation may seem deep and profound when you're tripping (believe me, I understand) but those musings don't always seem as profound when sober. <br /><br />In the first half of the book very little happens. Irulan wants her husband Paul to finally bang her and give her a baby, she hates Chani (Paul's first wife/"concubine"), Chani wants Paul's baby and Paul is tormented by his innate knowledge of all future events. But there is so much navel gazing... Where is the plot?<br /><br />Finally, in the last quarter of the book the action starts and it is amazing: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[the crazed antics of Bijaz the dwarf, Paul's blindness, Chani's death, the mentat clone Duncan/Hayt's transformation and his wonderful romance with Alia. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />In the beginning "Dune Messiah" was vague and ponderous, then sharply focused towards the spectacular end. I assume Herbert did this on purpose, as Paul experiences visions of all possible future-timelines. When he manipulates time to lead to the one future he did not see (his twins) this has deep ramifications. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[The loss of Chani and the birth of his "messianic" children leads to him now being truly, physically blind, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> while now we, the readers, see events unfold with crystal-clarity.<br /><br />I don't know. If only the rest of the book had been like that I would have loved this. But it was too uneven for a stellar rating.<br /><br /><strong>3 stars. The rating might improve upon a re-read, but I have so many books and life is short.</strong><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2599440494058985023">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12540852976017322310">Just getting my thoughts down about this. The last quarter of the book was amazing: Bijaz the dwarf, Paul's blindness, Chani's death, Duncan/Hayt's transformation, his romance with Alia... The plot was vague and ponderous at first, but then became sharply focused towards the end. I assume Herbert did this on purpose, as Paul's vision is clear with all possible future-timelines, so he manipulates them to lead to the one time he did not see (twins), making him truly blind, while we the reader see things unfold crystal-clear. I don't know. If only the rest of the book had been like that I would have loved this.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/727220947?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 22, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/24157203-haniel-barbosa" class="user" title="Haniel Barbosa">Haniel Barbosa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/24157203-haniel-barbosa?shelf=livros" class="actionLinkLite">livros</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer727220947">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14745240255551019215">Ler Duna pela primeira vez anos atrás foi uma experiência tão completa, tão recompensadora e impactante, que pensei não haver sentido em ler qualquer sequência. Em voltar a um lugar que já me dera tudo que poderia ser dado.<br /><br />Com o passar do tempo, no entanto, mais e mais pessoas me diziam que valia a pena ler os livros seguintes, que eles tinham o que acrescentar. Assim, reli Duna, revivendo todas as suas maravilhas, confirmando sua supremacia entre tudo que já li, e me preparei para continuar.<br /><br />Dur</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14745240255551019215">Ler Duna pela primeira vez anos atrás foi uma experiência tão completa, tão recompensadora e impactante, que pensei não haver sentido em ler qualquer sequência. Em voltar a um lugar que já me dera tudo que poderia ser dado.<br /><br />Com o passar do tempo, no entanto, mais e mais pessoas me diziam que valia a pena ler os livros seguintes, que eles tinham o que acrescentar. Assim, reli Duna, revivendo todas as suas maravilhas, confirmando sua supremacia entre tudo que já li, e me preparei para continuar.<br /><br />Durante muito tempo O Messias de Duna é decepcionante. Não por ser ruim, mas por Frank Herbert não conseguir contar tão bem a estória da maneira que escolheu. Mesmo assim, a partir de certo ponto o poder dos eventos, o poder dos personagens, o poder de todo o Universo de Duna, é tão grande, tão impactante, que não resta nada além de se deixar levar, de tentar viver ao máximo aquilo que lhe é oferecido. E dói. E atordoa. É magnífico.<br /><br />É um livro muito diferente do primeiro. Em tom, em ritmo, em o quão fundo vai na abordagem da natureza humana. Possui suas falhas, mas o seu auge é tão intenso que tenho apenas que lamentar ter durante tanto tempo evitado lê-lo. <br /><br />Obrigado, Frank Herbert. Que venham os próximos.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14745240255551019215">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  }

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/58.Frank_Herbert">About Frank Herbert</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/58.Frank_Herbert"><img alt="58" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1168661521p2/58.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer15154351498214686501">Frank Herbert was a critically acclaimed and commercially successful American science fiction author.<br /><br />He is best known for the novel <em>Dune</em> and its five sequels. The Dune saga, set in the distant future and taking place over millennia, dealt with themes such as human survival and evolution, ecology, and the intersection of religion, politics, and power, and is widely considered to be among the classi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15154351498214686501">Frank Herbert was a critically acclaimed and commercially successful American science fiction author.<br /><br />He is best known for the novel <em>Dune</em> and its five sequels. The Dune saga, set in the distant future and taking place over millennia, dealt with themes such as human survival and evolution, ecology, and the intersection of religion, politics, and power, and is widely considered to be among the classics in the field of science fiction.<br /><br />He was the father of fellow author <a title="Brian Herbert" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/56.Brian_Herbert">Brian Herbert</a>.</span>

The nearly invincible Honored Matres have swarmed into the known universe, driven from their home by a terrifying, mysterious Enemy. As designed by the creative genius of Frank Herbert, the primary story of Hunters and Sandworms is the exotic odyssey of Duncan\'s no-ship as it is forced to elude the diabolical traps set by the ferocious, unknown Enemy. To strengthen their forces, the fugitives have used genetic technology from Scytale, the last Tleilaxu Master, to revive key figures from Dune\'s past--including Paul Muad\'Dib and his beloved Chani, Lady Jessica, Stilgar, Thufir Hawat, and even Dr. Wellington Yueh.
